DRAFT: Appendix C expansion (Why not LoRA?):

We revisit the LoRA ablation with quantitative measurements. LoRA
specialists (Pythia-410M, $r=64$, $\alpha=128$, 2,000 steps, seed 42) achieve
mean divergence -20.31\% versus 15.65\% for full fine-tuning---a reduction
of 35.96pp. Under the empirical divergence-gain relationship
(Section~\ref{sec:divergence_gain}, $\approx$0.49$\times$ conversion rate on English
domains), this predicts a fusion gain of approximately -10.0\%.
The measured fusion gain is -13.85\%. The reduced divergence is the direct cause of the
reduced gain: LoRA's low-rank constraint limits the degree of domain adaptation,
keeping the specialist closer to the base model and reducing the complementarity
that drives MoE improvement. Full fine-tuning is required to achieve the divergence
levels (15.65\%) that produce the reported gains (7.72\%).

DRAFT: Appendix C expansion (Why not LoRA?):

We revisit the LoRA ablation with quantitative measurements. LoRA
specialists (Pythia-410M, $r=64$, $\alpha=128$, 2,000 steps, seed 42) achieve
mean divergence -20.54\% versus 15.65\% for full fine-tuning---a reduction
of 36.19pp. Under the empirical divergence-gain relationship
(Section~\ref{sec:divergence_gain}, $\approx$0.49$\times$ conversion rate on English
domains), this predicts a fusion gain of approximately -10.1\%.
The measured fusion gain is -14.01\%. The reduced divergence is the direct cause of the
reduced gain: LoRA's low-rank constraint limits the degree of domain adaptation,
keeping the specialist closer to the base model and reducing the complementarity
that drives MoE improvement. Full fine-tuning is required to achieve the divergence
levels (15.65\%) that produce the reported gains (7.72\%).
